The Jahmiyya Dilemma on Allah's Knowledge and the Eternity of Paradise and Hell
Nasafi answers two Jahmiyya objections on divine knowledge and equality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the Jahmiyya's dilemma that Allah's knowledge of the breaths of Paradise and Hell would imply their finitude, and why the unceasing existence of Paradise and Hell does not imply equality with Allah.

Core Idea
Allah knows the unnumbered breaths; created permanence is by preservation, not essential.

The Jahmiyya sect posed a tricky dilemma: if Allah knows the number of breaths in Paradise and Hell, then those breaths must be finite, implying the inhabitants eventually perish. But if He doesn't know, He is ignorant. Nasafi answers this challenge by affirming Allah's knowledge while denying the breaths are numbered. This passage then addresses a second objection—that unceasing existence would make creatures equal to Allah—clarifying the difference between essential and derived eternity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first dilemma
Read the first objection: the Jahmiyya force a choice between Allah's ignorance and the finitude of Paradise and Hell. Note the two horns of the dilemma.
This is a typical kalam argument by contradiction.
2
Nasafi's first answer
See how Nasafi escapes the dilemma: Allah knows the breaths, but they are not numbered and do not cease. This preserves both knowledge and endlessness.
The key is that 'knowing the number' does not apply if there is no number.
3
Examine the equality objection
The second objection claims that if creatures never cease, they share Allah's attribute of eternity. Identify the flaw Nasafi will expose.
4
Nasafi's refutation of equality
Read the answer: Allah is eternal by essence (no beginning, no end, not preserved), while creatures are created and remain only by Allah's preservation. Thus no equality.
The distinction is between necessary and contingent existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل
وفي سؤال الجهمية : إنَّ الله تعالى هل يعلم عدد أنفاس أهل الجنة والنار أم لا؟ فإن قلت: لا، فقد وصفت الله تعالى بالجهل. وإن قلت : نعم، فقد قلت بأن أهل الجنة والنار يفنيان
والجواب عنه أن نقول: إن الله تعالى يعلم أن أنفاس أهل الجنة والنار وليست بمعدودة ولا تنقطع.
فإن قيل: إذا قلتم بأنَّ أهل الجنة والنار لا يفنيان، فقد سوّيتم بينهم وبين الله تعالى. قلنا: لا يكون تسوية بينهم وبين الله تعالى، لأنَّ الله تعالى أوَّل قديم بلا ابتداء، وآخر حكيم بلا انتهاء، وأهل الجنة محدثون، وإنما يبقون ولا يفنون بإبقاء الله تعالى إيَّاهم، والله تعالى باقي لا بإبقاء أحد، فلا يكون تسوية بين الخالق والمخلوق.
Section
And regarding the question of the Jahmiyya: Does Allah, exalted, know the number of the breaths of the people of Paradise and Hell or not? If you say: No, then you have described Allah with ignorance. And if you say: Yes, then you have said that the people of Paradise and Hell cease to exist.
And the answer to it is that we say: Allah, exalted, knows that the breaths of the people of Paradise and Hell are not numbered and do not cease.
Then it is said: If you say that the people of Paradise and Hell do not cease to exist, then you have made them equal to Allah, exalted. We say: There is no equality between them and Allah, exalted, because Allah, exalted, is the First, the Eternal without beginning, and the Last, the Wise without end, and the people of Paradise are created, and they only remain and do not cease to exist by the preservation of Allah, exalted, of them, and Allah, exalted, is Eternal without being preserved by anyone, so there is no equality between the Creator and the created.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The Jahmiyya pose a dilemma: either Allah knows the number of breaths of the people of Paradise and Hell or He does not. If He does not, He is ignorant; if He does, then the breaths must be finite, implying the people cease to exist. Nasafi answers that Allah knows the breaths, but they are not numbered and do not cease. Then a second objection is raised: if the people of Paradise and Hell do not perish, then they are like Allah in eternal existence. Nasafi replies that equality is impossible because Allah is eternal without beginning or end, while the people of Paradise are created and only continue to exist by Allah's preservation. Allah's eternity is essential, not derived. Thus, no comparison between Creator and creature.

Key Terms
الجهمية
أنفاس
باقي
إبقاء
تسوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the unceasing existence of Paradise and Hell implies equality with Allah.
Correction: Creatures remain only by Allah's preservation; Allah is eternal by His own essence, without need of preservation. Hence no equality.

Checkpoint
How does Nasafi avoid the dilemma that if Allah knows the number of breaths of Paradise and Hell, then their number must be finite?
A) He says the breaths are not numbered and do not cease.
B) He says Allah does not know the number.
C) He says the breaths are infinite but known as a whole.
D) He says the question is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Allah's knowledge encompasses all things, even those without number, and the permanence of Paradise and Hell is by divine preservation, not by inherent eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah’s knowledge of every breath you take is not limited by number or time, does that make you more aware of His watchfulness over your every moment? How can you live today with the certainty that your existence is upheld by Him at every instant, not by your own power?
?
The people of Paradise depend entirely on Allah's preservation to remain. Reflect: in what areas of your life do you still rely on your own perceived independence rather than acknowledging that your very being is a gift sustained by Allah?
?
The Jahmiyya assumed that if something is known, it must be finite. Where in your life have you placed limits on Allah’s knowledge or power, thinking He cannot handle the vastness of your struggles or the depth of your sins? How would your prayers change if you truly believed He knows all without limit?

Mu'tazilite View of Divine Justice
Nasafi presents the Mu'tazilite argument on divine justice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that God does not create evil because it would contradict His justice.

Core Idea
God's justice means He cannot be the source of evil.

The Mu'tazilites placed great emphasis on God's justice, arguing that it logically restricts His actions. In this passage, Nasafi presents their core reasoning: if God created evil and then punished people for it, He would be unjust. Let's examine this argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how the Mu'tazilites connect God's justice to the absence of created evil.
The passage uses a conditional (law) to highlight the contradiction.
2
Identify the key terms
Find 'al-ʿadl' (justice) and 'al-sharr' (evil). Understand how they are linked in the argument.
3
Reflect on the reasoning
Ask yourself: Why do the Mu'tazilites think creating evil would be injustice? What does this imply about God's nature?
This reasoning distinguishes them from the Ashʿarites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما العدل: قالوا بأن الله تعالى لا يخلق الشر، ولا يقضى به؛ لأنه لو خلق الشر وقضى به ثم يعذبهم على ذلك يكون ذلك منه جورا، والله تعالى عادل لا يجور. وأما الثاني: قالوا بأن القرآن مخلوق وكذا سائر صفاته؛ لأنا لو قلنا بأنه غير مخلوق لا يكون توحيدا.
As for justice: they said that God most high does not create evil, nor decree it; because if He created evil and decreed it then punished them for that, that would be injustice from Him, and God most high is just and does not commit injustice. As for the second: they said that the Qur'an is created, and likewise the rest of His attributes; because if we say that it is uncreated, there would be no oneness.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite understanding of the first two principles. First, al-'Adl (divine justice): They assert that God cannot create or decree evil, because doing so and then punishing His creatures for that evil would be unjust (jūr). Since God is absolutely just, He cannot be the source of evil. Second, al-Tawḥīd (divine oneness): They argue that the Qur'an is created (makhliq) and likewise all other divine attributes. Their reasoning is that if the Qur'an were uncreated (ghayr makhliq), it would imply a co-eternal entity with God, thus compromising strict oneness. This is a foundational Mu'tazilite doctrine distinguishing them from the Ahl al-Sunnah who affirm the uncreatedness of the Qur'an.

Key Terms
العدل
الشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilites deny God's power over all things.
Correction: They affirm His power but argue that His justice limits what He chooses to do; He is capable of evil but would not do it because it is unjust.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilites in this passage, why can't God create evil?
A) Because evil is nonexistent in reality.
B) Because if He did and then punished for it, that would be injustice.
C) Because God is not powerful enough to create evil.
D) Because evil comes from Satan, not from God.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites base their doctrine of divine justice on the logical incompatibility of God creating evil and then punishing for it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face personal suffering or witness evil in the world, do you ever secretly blame God or feel He has been unfair? What would it mean to take the Mu'tazilite position seriously—that God does not create evil—and instead examine your own actions or the actions of others? Will you commit to refraining from questioning God's justice when hardship comes your way?
?
The Mu'tazilites argued that if God created evil and then punished for it, that would be injustice. Have you ever held God responsible for a tragedy, thinking He should not have allowed it? How does this lesson challenge your assumptions about God's role in evil? What concrete step can you take to align your heart with the belief that God is always just, even when you don't understand?
?
This passage uses a logical proof to defend God's justice. Do you rely more on reason or on scripture when forming your beliefs about God? If reason leads to a conclusion that seems to conflict with other verses (e.g., that God creates everything), how do you reconcile them? Are you willing to trust that God's justice is perfect, even if your mind cannot fully grasp it?

Mu'tazilite View on the Createdness of the Qur'an
Nasafi presents the Mu'tazilite argument for the createdness of the Qur'an.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite argument that the Qur'an must be created to preserve God's absolute oneness.

Core Idea
If the Qur'an were eternal, it would violate tawḥīd.

The second Mu'tazilite principle discussed by Nasafi concerns divine oneness (tawḥīd). They argue that affirming the Qur'an as uncreated would imply a co-eternal entity with God, thereby compromising strict monotheism. Let's see how they reason this.

Mu'tazilite and Sunni Views on Promise and Threat
Nasafi contrasts Mu'tazilite and Sunni positions on God's threat and presents scriptural debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the contrasting positions of the Mu'tazilites and Sunnis regarding God's threat of punishment and the scriptural basis for the Sunni view.

Core Idea
God's mercy permits forgiveness of threats, except for shirk.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi presents two pivotal Mu'tazilite principles — that God must fulfill both promises and threats — and the Sunni response. The debate hinges on whether God is obliged to punish every threatened sin or whether His will and mercy can override such threats. The Mu'tazilites cite verses threatening eternal punishment, while the Sunnis appeal to the exception of shirk in the Qur'an.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two positions
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two Mu'tazilite principles and the Sunni counter-position.
The passage is structured as a contrast.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite principle of no breaking
The Mu'tazilites argue that God cannot break a promise or a threat because that would be impossible (khulf) in His speech.
This is based on rational necessity.
3
Learn the Sunni concession on threats
The Sunnis agree that God must keep promises but disagree on threats: He may choose to forgive instead of punish.
4
Examine the scriptural evidence
The Mu'tazilites quote verses threatening eternal Hell for murder. The Sunnis counter with the verse that only shirk is unforgivable, implying other sins may be forgiven.
Qur'an 4:48 is the key Sunni proof.
5
Grasp the final objection and conclusion
The Mu'tazilites object that forgiveness would be 'breaking' the threat. The Sunni answer implies that the threat is conditional or excepted.
The passage ends with the Mu'tazilite objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثالث: قالوا بأن الله تعالى إذا وعد عباده ثوابا لا يجوز أن يخالف وعده؛ لأن الله تعالى لا يخلف الميعاد. والرابع: وإذا أوعد وعيدا لا يجوز أن لا يعذبهم ويخالف وعيده؛ لأن الخلف في كلام الله تعالى لا يجوز. وقال أهل السنة والجماعة: إن الله تعالى إذا أوعد وعيدا يجوز أن لا يعذبهم، ولكنه يعفو ويغفر لهم ولا يعاقبهم. واحتجت المعتزلة بقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَمَن يَقْتُلْ مُؤْمِنًا مُتَعَمِّدًا فَجَزَاؤُهُ جَهَنَّمُ خَلِدًا فِيهَا وَغَضِبَ اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَلَعَنَهُ وَأَعَدَّ لَهُ عَذَابًا عَظِيمًا ﴾ [النساء: ٩٣] وكذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَن يَفْعَلْ ذَلِكَ عُدْوَانًا وَظُلْمًا فَسَوْفَ نُصْلِيهِ نَارًا ﴾ [النساء: ٣٠]. والجواب عنه قلنا ما ذكر الله تعالى من الوعيد صار مستثنى بقوله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّ اللَّهَ لا يَغْفِرُ أَن يُشْرَكَ بِهِ وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَن يَشَاءُ ﴾ [النساء: ٤٨]. وقولهم: يكون خلفا في الوعيد.
As for the third: they said that God most high, when He promises His servants a reward, it is not permissible for Him to break His promise, because God most high does not break the appointment. And the fourth: when He threatens a threat, it is not permissible for Him not to punish them and break His threat, because breaking [promise] in the speech of God most high is not permissible. The people of the Sunnah and the community said: God most high, when He threatens a threat, it is permissible for Him not to punish them, but He pardons and forgives them and does not punish them. The Mu'tazilites argued with His statement: 'And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is Hell, abiding therein, and God's wrath is upon him, and He has cursed him and prepared for him a great punishment.' (4:93) and likewise His statement: 'And whoever does that in hostility and injustice, We will soon roast him in a fire' (4:30). The answer to that: we said that what God mentioned of threat is excepted by His statement: 'Indeed, God does not forgive associating partners with Him, but He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' (4:48). And their saying: it would be a breaking of the threat.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite third and fourth principles—promise (al-wa'd) and threat (al-wa'īd)—and the Sunni response. The Mu'tazilites argue that God must fulfill both promises of reward and threats of punishment, because 'breaking' (al-khulf) is impossible in God's speech. The Ahl al-Sunnah counter that while God must keep promises, He may forgive threats; He is not obliged to punish every threatened sin. The Mu'tazilites cite Quranic verses threatening eternal punishment for murder. The Sunni response argues that such threats are excepted by the verse on shirk (4:48), which indicates that less than shirk may be forgiven. The last phrase, 'And their saying: it would be a breaking of the threat,' likely concludes the Mu'tazilite objection. This exchange highlights the Sunni emphasis on God's mercy and will in punishment.

Key Terms
الوعد
الوعيد
أهل السنة والجماعة
الخلف في الوعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Sunni position means God never punishes threats.
Correction: The Sunnis affirm that God may punish but also may forgive, except for shirk.

Checkpoint
According to the Ahl al-Sunnah in this passage, why do threatened punishments not necessarily apply to all sinners?
A) Because God breaks His promises.
B) Because the threat is conditional on shirk.
C) Because the threat is excepted by the verse on shirk, allowing forgiveness for lesser sins.
D) Because God never threatens punishment.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position distinguishes between God's promise (which must be kept) and God's threat (which may be forgiven), based on the exception of shirk in Qur'an 4:48.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a sin you have committed that you secretly believe is too great for God to forgive, even though this lesson teaches that only shirk is unforgivable? How does that belief affect your relationship with Allah today?
?
When you sin, do you immediately despair of God's mercy, acting as if the Mu'tazilite view is true and punishment is inevitable? Or do you truly rely on the Sunni position that God may forgive? Which pattern dominates your heart?
?
The Mu'tazilites insisted that God must punish every threat because He cannot break His word. But God's word includes the exception of shirk. Are there areas in your life where you hold God to a standard of strict justice for yourself or others, forgetting His mercy? How can you change that starting today?

The Distinction Between Promise and Threat and the Interpretation of the Killing Verse
Imam Nasafi responds to an objection and interprets a verse on killing a believer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah's forgiveness does not break His threat, and how the verse on killing a believer is interpreted to apply only when the killer considers it lawful.

Core Idea
Forgiveness is generosity, not a broken threat; the verse targets those who deem killing lawful.

Imam Nasafi here tackles a crucial objection: if Allah forgives a murderer, doesn't that break His threat of eternal punishment? He clarifies the difference between a promise (which must be kept) and a threat (which can be lifted by grace). He then reinterprets the famous verse from Surah An-Nisa, showing that its severe warning is not for every intentional killer but specifically for those who consider the killing lawful (istihlal), as illustrated by the story of Miqyas ibn Sababa.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Notice the shift from a general principle to a specific verse and a historical example.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the distinction between promise and threat
Imam Nasafi argues that breaking a promise is blameworthy because it denies a right, but showing mercy by pardoning a threat is noble. Identify the key terms: wa'd (promise) and wa'id (threat).
This is a core theological principle in Sunni kalam.
3
Analyze the interpretation of the verse
The author gives two interpretations: first, Ibn Abbas's gloss that 'his recompense is Hell' means it is his just due but forgiveness is possible; second, that the verse refers to those who deem killing lawful. Notice the evidence from the story of Miqyas.
The story shows the extreme case of apostasy combined with murder.
4
Consider the implications for Islamic theology
Reflect on how this interpretation balances divine justice and mercy. The threat remains real for the obstinate, but not for the repentant believer who acknowledges the sin.
This relates to the Maturidi position on grave sinners.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا: لا يكون ذلك خلفاً في الوعيد، بل يعفو عنه كرماً وفضلاً، بخلاف ما إذا وعد الثواب حيث لا يجوز أن يخلف وعده؛ لأن ذلك حق العبد؛ فلو جاز ذلك يكون لوماً ولا يُعدُّ كرمًا، وهذا لا يُظن بالله عز وجل. والجواب عن قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَمَن يَقْتُلْ مُؤْمِنًا مُتَعَمِّدًا فَجَزَاؤُهُ جَهَنَّمُ خَلِدًا فِيهَا وَغَضِبَ اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَلَعَنَهُ وَأَعَدَّ لَهُ عَذَابًا عَظِيمًا ﴾ [النساء: ٩٣]. قال ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما: فَجَزَاؤُهُ جَهَنَّمُ : أي جزاه. يدل عليه قوله تعالى: ﴿ يَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الْقِصَاصُ فِي الْقَتْلَى ﴾ [البقرة: ۱۷۸] فسماه مؤمنًا بعد قَتْلِ العَمْدِ، على أنا نقول: أراد به إذا استحل قتل المؤمنين. وقد رُوي أنَّ الآية نزلت في حقٌّ مِقْيس بن صبابة الكناني، حين قتل مسلما من بني فهر بعدما قتل أخوه هشام بن صبابة وارتد ولحق بدار الحرب، والدليل على ارتداده قوله في شعره قتلتُ بني فهر، وجملت عقله سراة بني النَّجَّار أربابُ قَارِعِ شَفَيْتُ به نفسي وأدركْتُ مُنْيتي وكنتُ إلى الأوثان أوَّلُ راجع ومن قتل مؤمناً متعمداً واستحلَّ قتله كما استحل مقيس بن صبابة يكون كافراً ويخلد في النار مع سائر الكفار
We said: That is not a breach of the threat; rather He pardons him as generosity and grace, contrary to when He promises reward, for it is not permissible to break His promise, because that is the right of the servant; if that were permissible, it would be blameworthy and not considered generosity, and this is not attributed to Allah Almighty. And the answer to His statement, the Exalted: 'And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is Hell, abiding therein eternally, and Allah has become angry with him and has cursed him and has prepared for him a great punishment' [An-Nisa 4:93]. Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with them both, said: 'His recompense is Hell' i.e., his requital. That is indicated by His statement: 'O you who have believed, prescribed for you is legal retribution for those murdered' [Al-Baqarah 2:178]; thus He called him a believer after intentional killing. Moreover, we say: He intended by it when he considers the killing of believers lawful. And it has been narrated that the verse was revealed concerning Miqyas ibn Sababa al-Kinani, when he killed a Muslim from Banu Fihr after his brother Hisham ibn Sababa had been killed, and he apostatized and joined the abode of war. The evidence for his apostasy is his saying in his poetry: 'I killed Banu Fihr, and collected his blood-money, the chiefs of Banu al-Najjar, lords of Qari'; I healed myself thereby and achieved my desire, and I was the first to return to idols.' And whoever kills a believer intentionally and considers his killing lawful, as Miqyas ibn Sababa considered it lawful, becomes a disbeliever and will abide eternally in the Fire with the rest of the disbelievers.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection regarding the threat of eternal punishment. The objection is that if Allah forgives a believer who kills intentionally, He would be breaking His threat. The answer begins by stating that forgiveness is not a breach because it is an act of generosity and grace. The difference between threat and promise is explained: breaking a promise of reward would be blameworthy because it is a right of the servant, whereas the threat is a right of Allah, and He may pardon out of grace. Then the verse from An-Nisa is cited. The author provides two interpretations: first, Ibn Abbas explains that 'his recompense is Hell' means his requital (i.e., it is his just due, but Allah may forgive). Second, the verse is interpreted as referring to one who considers killing a believer lawful, like Miqyas ibn Sababa, who apostatized and killed a Muslim, thus becoming a disbeliever. The story of Miqyas is given to illustrate that the verse applies to those who deem it lawful, not to every intentional killer.

Key Terms
الوعيد
الخلف
العفو
الكرم
الاستحلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every Muslim who intentionally kills another believer is eternally damned without possibility of forgiveness.
Correction: The verse applies only to those who consider the killing lawful (istihlal), not to those who acknowledge it as a sin and seek forgiveness.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Nasafi, what is the key condition for the eternal punishment mentioned in Surah An-Nisa verse 93?
A) The killer must be a disbeliever.
B) The killer must have intentionally killed a believer and not repented.
C) The killer must consider the killing lawful (istihlal).
D) The killer must have killed multiple believers.

Key Takeaway
The eternal threat in the verse is reserved for those who consider killing a believer lawful, not for every intentional killer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a major sin, do you secretly fear that Allah's threat must be fulfilled without exception? How does the distinction between promise and threat change your understanding of His mercy and your own hope for forgiveness? What will you do today to turn back to Him with sincerity?
?
Have you ever justified a sin in your heart, convincing yourself it is 'not that bad' or that your circumstances make it permissible? This is the very mindset of istihlal. Identify one area where you have done this and commit to seeking knowledge to correct it.
?
The story of Miqyas shows how a single act of deeming murder lawful led him to apostasy and return to idols. What attachments, habits, or beliefs in your life are slowly pulling you away from submission? Are you willing to cut them off before they take root?

The Revealed Books and the Ruling on Denying Them
Nasafi enumerates all revealed books and their recipients, and the ruling on denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the complete list of revealed books (suhuf and major books) according to Imam Nasafi, and the rulings on denying any part of them.

Core Idea
All revealed books are the created speech of Allah, and denying any is disbelief.

Imam Nasafi begins by affirming that all revealed books are the created speech of Allah, establishing the Ash'ari position. He then provides a detailed enumeration of the hundred suhuf (scrolls) and four major books, specifying their recipients. Finally, he outlines the serious consequences of denying any verse from these books, including a nuanced ruling on denying an unnamed messenger.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's full message.
Notice the precise numbers and names.
2
Identify the total number of suhuf and books
Find the total count of suhuf (100) and major books (4). Notice how they are distributed among the prophets.
The suhuf are 50 to Shiyth, 30 to Idris, 10 to Ibrahim, 10 to Musa.
3
Trace the order of revelation and recipients
List the four major books in order: Tawrah, Zabur, Injil, Furqan. Note who each was revealed to and the timeline (e.g., Tawrah after Pharaoh's drowning).
The Furqan (Quran) is the last and to Muhammad, the last messenger.
4
Understand the ruling on denial
The passage states that denying any verse from these books is disbelief (kufr). Also, denying a messenger not explicitly named leads to innovation, not disbelief, unless apostasy.
The nuance: if the person entered Islam formally, denial of an unnamed messenger makes him an apostate.
5
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider why this enumeration matters: it affirms the divine origin of all scriptures and sets clear boundaries for belief.
This connects to the Ash'ari position that the Quran is created.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يجب أن تعرف أن جميع الكتب التي أنزلها الله تعالى على الأنبياء والرسل كلام الله مخلوق، وذلك مئة صحيفة وأربعة كتب خمسون منها أنزلها الله تعالى على شَيْتَ بن آدم عليهما السلام، وثلاثون صحيفة علي إدريس صلوات الله عليه، وعشر مصاحف على إبراهيم عليه السلام، وعشر مصاحف على موسى عليه السلام قبل نزول التوراة وتُسَمَّى كتاب الستة، وكان قبل غرق فرعون عليه اللعنة. ثم أنزل الله تعالى التوراة بعد غرق فرعون، ثم أنزل الله تعالى الزبور على داود عليه السلام، ثم أنزل الله تعالى الإنجيل على عيسي عليه السلام، وهو آخر الأنبياء من بني إسرائيل، ثم أنزل الفرقان علي محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وهو آخر الرُّسُل. فكل من أنكر آيةً من هذه الكتب فإنه يكفر، وإذا قال: آمنت بجميع الرسل ثم أنكر واحدًا من الرسل الذي ليس بمنصوص عليه، وقال: هذا ليس منهم لا يكفر ويكون مبتدعاً، هذا إذا لم يدخل في دين من الأديان، أما إذا دخل في دين من الأديان يكون مرتدّا فيقتل.
You must know that all the books which Allah Most High revealed to the prophets and messengers are the created speech of Allah. That is one hundred suhuf and four books: fifty of them were revealed by Allah Most High to Shiyth son of Adam, peace be upon them; thirty suhuf to Idris, may the blessings of Allah be upon him; ten mushafs to Ibrahim, peace be upon him; and ten mushafs to Musa, peace be upon him, before the revelation of the Tawrah, and they are called the Book of the Six, and that was before the drowning of Pharaoh, upon him be the curse. Then Allah Most High revealed the Tawrah after the drowning of Pharaoh. Then Allah Most High revealed the Zabur to Dawud, peace be upon him. Then Allah Most High revealed the Injil to Isa, peace be upon him, and he is the last of the prophets from the Children of Israel. Then He revealed the Furqan (the Quran) to Muhammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and he is the last of the messengers. So whoever denies a verse from these books then he disbelieves. And if someone says: 'I believe in all the messengers' but then denies one of the messengers who is not explicitly mentioned, and says: 'This one is not among them,' he does not disbelieve but becomes an innovator. This is if he has not entered a religion. If he has entered a religion, then he becomes an apostate and is killed.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and doctrinal ruling regarding the revealed books and messengers. It begins with a theological statement: all revealed books are the created speech of Allah (كلام الله مخلوق). This affirms the Ash'ari position that the Quran is created. Then it gives the total number of revelations: one hundred suhuf (scrolls) plus four books. The suhuf are allocated: 50 to Shiyth, 30 to Idris, 10 to Ibrahim, and 10 to Musa (before the Tawrah). These ten suhuf of Musa are called the 'Book of the Six' (كتاب الستة) and were revealed before the drowning of Pharaoh. Then the four major books are listed in order: Tawrah after Pharaoh's drowning, Zabur to Dawud, Injil to Isa (last of Israelite prophets), and the Furqan (Quran) to Muhammad (last of all messengers). The passage then gives rulings: denying any verse from these books is disbelief. Then a nuanced case: if one professes belief in all messengers but denies a specific messenger not mentioned by name (i.e., not one of the 25 or well-known ones), he is not a disbeliever but an innovator. However, if that person had formally entered a religion (i.e., Islam), then denying such a messenger makes him an apostate deserving death. This shows the gravity of denying any part of divine revelation.

Quranic Proof for Belief in All Books and Messengers
Nasafi cites Quranic proofs that belief in all books and messengers is obligatory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Quran requires believers to affirm all divine books and all prophets as a condition of faith.

Core Idea
Belief in all books and messengers is not optional—it is a condition.

This passage presents two Quranic proofs that establish the obligation to believe in all of Allah's books and all of His messengers. Nasafi uses direct verses to show that faith is not selective; it requires comprehensive affirmation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two conditions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting that it states two separate conditions: belief in all books and belief in all messengers.
The passage is short but powerful.
2
Examine the first verse (2:136)
Look at the verse: 'Say: We believe in Allah and what has been sent down to us.' This commands belief in all revelations, not just some.
The phrase 'what has been sent down to us' implies all divine books.
3
Examine the second verse (2:177)
The verse lists belief in the Book and the prophets as components of righteousness, alongside belief in Allah and the Last Day.
This shows that believing in all prophets is part of true faith.
4
Reflect on the necessity of comprehensive belief
Ask yourself: why does faith require believing in all books and messengers? Consider that rejecting one is like rejecting all.
The Quran emphasizes unity of the message.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على أن الإيمان بجميع الكتب شرط قال الله تعالى: ﴿ قُولُوا ءَامَنَا بِاللَّهِ وَمَا أُنزِلَ إِلَيْنَا ﴾ [البقرة: ١٣٦] الآية والإيمان بجميع الرسل شرط، قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَلَكِنَّ البِرَّ مَنْ ءَامَنَ بِاللَّهِ وَالْيَوْمِ الْآخِرِ وَالْمَلَائِكَةِ وَالْكِتَابِ وَالنَّبِيِّينَ ﴾ [البقرة: ١٧٧].
The proof that belief in all books is a condition: Allah said: 'Say: We believe in Allah and what has been sent down to us' [2:136] the verse. And belief in all messengers is a condition: Allah said: 'But righteousness is [he] who believes in Allah, the Last Day, the angels, the Book, and the prophets' [2:177].
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage presents Quranic evidence that faith requires believing in all of Allah's books and messengers. The author cites two verses: the first (2:136) commands believers to affirm belief in what was revealed to them, which implicitly includes all divine books; the second (2:177) lists belief in the angels, the Book, and the prophets alongside Allah and the Last Day as a component of true righteousness. Thus the obligatory nature of believing in all books and all messengers is established. The structure is straightforward: proof followed by the condition (shart).

Key Takeaway
The Quran establishes that true faith requires believing in all of Allah's books and all of His messengers.

Wine Forbidden by Revelation and Response to Objection
Imam al-Nasafi argues for wine prohibition and answers an objection about timing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural evidence for the prohibition of wine and how a potential objection about the timing of the revelation is answered.

Core Idea
Wine is forbidden by explicit prophetic report and Quranic identification of sin as wine.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi presents two key issues: the scriptural basis for the prohibition of wine and a response to an objection concerning a verse that seems to excuse past consumption. Additionally, a brief ruling on beating the drum in weddings is given. We'll explore how the author uses hadith, Quran, and poetry to establish the ruling, and then addresses the historical context of the revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the flow. Notice the sequence: a Quranic verse, then the claim that wine is forbidden, followed by evidence, an objection, and a brief ruling on drums.
The passage starts with a verse about creation, which sets the context of accountability.
2
Identify the evidence for prohibition
Find the hadith that prohibits wine (including its little and much) and the Quranic verse that lists 'sin' (ithm) as forbidden. Note how the author equates ithm with wine using a line of poetry.
The evidence is both from the Prophet's statement and the Quran.
3
Understand the objection and its answer
Some argued that the prohibition verse (Al-A'raf 33) was revealed after people had drunk wine, implying it was not yet forbidden. The author explains that verse was a comfort for those who drank unknowingly after the prohibition was revealed but before they learned of it.
This shows that rulings apply only after knowledge reaches the people.
4
Note the ruling on drum beating
The author mentions that beating the drum is permitted by Imam al-Shafi'i in weddings for announcement, not for entertainment. This is a separate point from the main argument.
This illustrates how permissibility is context-dependent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعالى: (أَفَحَسِبْتُمْ أَنَّمَا خَلَقْنَاكُمْ عَبَثًا وَأَنَّكُمْ إِلَيْنَا لَا تُرْجَعُونَ) [المؤمنون: 115]. وأما قولنا: الخمر حرام؛ لأنه ورد بها الخبر، وهو قوله : الحرمت عليكم الخمر قليلها وكثيرها والسكر من كل شراب. وقال الله تعالى: ﴿ قُلْ إِنَّمَا حَرَّمَ رَبِّيَ الْفَوَاحِشَ مَا ظَهَرَ مِنْهَا وَمَا بَطَنَ وَالإِثْمَ وَالْبَغْيَ بِغَيْرِ الْحَقِّ وَأَن تُشْرِكُوا بِاللَّهِ مَا لَمْ يُنزِلْ بِهِ سُلْطَانًا وَأَن تَقُولُوا عَلَى اللَّهِ مَا لَا تَعْلَمُونَ ﴾ [الأعراف: 33] والإثم هو الخمر، يدل عليه قول القائل: شربت الخمر حتى ضل عقلي كذاك الإثم تذهب بالعقول. والجواب عن احتجاجهم بالآية، قلنا: الآية نزلت في قوم شربوا الخمر بعد نزول آية التحريم قبل بلوغ الخبر إليهم، فاغتموا بذلك فأنزل الله تعالى هذه الآية. وأما ضرب الدف، قلنا: أباحه الإمام الشافعي رضي الله عنه في التزويج للإعلام لا للعب.
Exalted is He: 'Did you think that We created you in play and that you would not be returned to Us?' [Al-Mu'minun: 115]. As for our statement: Wine is forbidden because the report came concerning it, and it is his saying: 'Wine is forbidden to you, its little and its much, and the intoxicant from every drink.' And Allah the Exalted said: 'Say: My Lord has only forbidden immoralities – what is apparent of them and what is concealed – and sin, and oppression without right, and that you associate with Allah that for which He has not sent down authority, and that you say about Allah that which you do not know' [Al-A'raf: 33]. And 'sin' (al-ithm) is wine, as indicated by the saying of the poet: 'I drank wine until my mind strayed; thus sin (al-ithm) takes away intellects.' And the answer to their argument by the verse: We say: The verse was revealed concerning a people who drank wine after the revelation of the verse of prohibition before the news reached them, and they were distressed by that, so Allah the Exalted revealed this verse. And as for beating the drum, we say: Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him, permitted it in marriage for announcement, not for play.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument defending the prohibition of wine by revelation and responding to an objection about the timing of a Quranic verse. The author begins by citing a Quranic verse (Al-Mu'minun 115) to establish the purpose of creation, then states that wine is forbidden because a prophetic report (hadith) has come regarding it, quoting the Prophet's prohibition of wine and intoxicants. He then cites another verse (Al-A'raf 33) where Allah prohibits sins, and identifies 'sin' (ithm) as wine, supporting this with a line of poetry. The author then addresses an objection: some argued that the prohibition verse was revealed after people had already drunk wine, implying that it was not yet forbidden. He answers that the verse Abu 'l-Laith al-Samarqandi? actually pertains to those who drank after the prohibition was revealed but before they learned of it, and that the verse was sent down to relieve their distress. Finally, he discusses the ruling on beating the drum (duff), stating that Imam al-Shafi'i permitted it in weddings for announcement, not for amusement. The passage demonstrates how Islamic law relies on scripture and the principle that rulings apply only after knowledge of them reaches the people.

Correction: The author clarifies that the verse was a comfort to those who drank after the prohibition was revealed but before they heard about it, so the ruling was already in place; ignorance of the prohibition excuses sin.

Key Takeaway
The prohibition of wine is established by clear revelation, and rulings apply only after they are known; ignorance of the ruling can excuse past actions.

Defending Abrogation Against the Badā' Objection
Imam al-Nasafi refutes the claim that abrogation implies badā' (change of mind) in Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic scholars refute the claim that abrogation implies a change of mind (badā') in Allah, using the concept of temporary rulings and the analogy of resurrection.

Core Idea
Abrogation is not a change of mind but the end of a temporary ruling.

This lesson addresses a critical theological objection: if Allah can abrogate a previously permitted ruling, does that mean He changes His mind? The objection seems logical, but the answer reveals a deeper understanding of divine decree. Imam al-Nasafi shows that abrogation simply means a preordained temporary ruling has reached its term, just as the resurrection ends the state of death.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the objector's premise: abrogation would mean Allah 'returns' from His first command, implying ignorance.
2
Identify the key claim in the objection
The objector says badā' and return are only from one who is ignorant of outcomes.
3
Read the answer: abrogation as expiration
The author rejects that abrogation involves badā'; rather, it is the expiration of a temporary ruling that was always limited in time.
The key term is 'munqadā' – expiration.
4
Follow the analogy of resurrection
Allah will resurrect the dead; this is not called badā' but the end of death and start of new life. Similarly for abrogation.
5
Reflect on why this matters
This clarifies that Allah's knowledge and will are unchanged; He decrees temporary rulings from eternity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: إباحة الخمر والمتعة كانت حلالاً في الابتداء، فلو قلنا بجواز النسخ يكون ذلك رجوعًا عن الأول، ويصير كأن الله تعالى أمر بأمر، ثم بدا له الرجوع عن ذلك، والبداء والرجوع من الله تعالى لا يصح؛ لأن البداء والرجوع إنما يكون ممن كان جاهلاً ولا يعرف عواقب الأمور. والجواب عنه أن نقول: لا نسلم بأن في النسخ بداءً ورجوعًا، بل هو انقضاء حكم أول وانتهاؤه، واستئناف حكم آخر؛ لأنه قد ظهر لنا أن الحكم الأول [قد انتهى] لم يكن مؤبدًا، لكنه مؤقت إلى ذلك الوقت، إلا أنا لا نعرف ذلك، فظهر لنا أن الحكم الأول قد انتهى وانقضى. يدل عليه أن الله تعالى يحشر الموتى يوم القيامة، ولا يقال بأن فيه بداءً ورجوعًا، بل فيه انتهاء حكم الموتى، ويستأنف حكمًا آخر، كذلك ههنا ولا يقال بأن في النسخ بداءً ورجوعاً، بل فيه انتهاء حكم المنسوخ في الموتى، واستئناف حكم الناسخ.
For if it is said: The permissibility of wine and temporary marriage (mut'ah) were lawful in the beginning, and if we say that abrogation (naskh) is permissible, that would be a return from the first, and it would become as if Allah the Exalted commanded a command, then He changed His mind about that, and badā' (change of mind) and return from Allah the Exalted are not valid because badā' and return are only from one who is ignorant and does not know the outcomes of affairs. The answer to that is that we say: We do not concede that in abrogation there is badā' and return; rather, it is the expiration of a first ruling and its termination, and the initiation of another ruling. Because it became apparent to us that the first ruling [has ended] was not eternal, but was temporary until that time, except that we did not know that. So it became apparent to us that the first ruling has ended and terminated. Evidence for that is that Allah the Exalted will resurrect the dead on the Day of Resurrection, and it is not said that in that there is badā' and return. Rather, there is the termination of the ruling of the dead, and He initiates another ruling. Similarly here: It is not said that in abrogation there is badā' and return; rather, there is the termination of the abrogated ruling in the dead, and the initiation of the abrogating ruling.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the Islamic doctrine of abrogation (naskh). The objector claims that if we accept that Allah can abrogate a previously permitted ruling (like wine and temporary marriage), this would imply that Allah changes His mind (badā'), which is impossible because change of mind occurs only from ignorance of future outcomes. The author responds by rejecting that abrogation involves badā'. He explains that abrogation is simply the expiration of a temporary ruling that was always intended to end at a specific time, even though humans were not aware of its time limit. When the appointed time arrives, the ruling ends and a new one begins. To illustrate, he gives the analogy of the resurrection: Allah will resurrect the dead on the Day of Judgment; this is not considered a change of mind but the end of the state of death and the beginning of new life. Likewise, abrogation is the end of a temporary ruling and the start of another. The author emphasizes that no one accuses Allah of badā' in resurrection, so the same applies to abrogation.

Key Terms
النسخ
البداء
الرجوع
المؤقت
الحكم الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abrogation implies that Allah changed His mind (badā'), which would be a flaw.
Correction: Abrogation is simply the end of a pre-ordained temporary ruling; Allah always knew the time limit and intended the new ruling from eternity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does abrogation represent instead of badā'?
A) A contradiction in divine commands
B) The expiration of a temporary ruling and initiation of a new one
C) An error in the original ruling
D) A concession to human weakness

Key Takeaway
Abrogation does not imply a change in Allah's will; it is simply the expiration of a temporary ruling and the start of a new one, as with the resurrection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where a religious ruling seems to have changed, do you trust that Allah's wisdom is perfect, or do you secretly doubt His consistency? How does this lesson on abrogation as expiration of a temporary ruling challenge that doubt?
?
Have you ever felt that Allah 'changed His mind' about something in your life—like a prayer answered differently than expected? How does the distinction between badā' and the expiration of a temporary decree correct that thought?
?
If you truly believed that every apparent change in your life is not a sign of Allah's uncertainty but the end of a preordained temporary phase, how would that transform your patience and hope in trials?
